```
77  theAccount object reference GenAcct.

invoke GenAcct 'setBank'(bank-nbr)
invoke GenAcct 'setAccount(account)
invoke GenAcct 'setGetBalance'(true)
invoke GenAcct 'setGetNameAddr'(false)  *> not really needed, since it would
default to false, but just to show that it's something that could be
optionally done.
set theAccount to GenAcct::"getAcctMaster"()
move theAccount::"type"() to output-acct-type
move theAccount::"status"() to output-acct-status
move theAccount::"currentBalance"() to output-current-bal
move theAccount::"availableBalance"() to output-avail-bal

Ah, if only!

I considered using properties instead of methods, but the requirement to
declare them in the repository makes that, umm, inconvenient, especially
when there are dozens or even hundreds of them.  A shame, because it looks
to be a nice feature.  Micro Focus allows you to use the "inline invocation
operator" to access properties, like so:

move bank-nbr to GenAcct::"bank"
*> or
set GenAcct::"bank" to bank-nbr

Which is pretty nice, though it does make it hard to tell the difference
between a property and a method with no parameters (which is why I use empty
pararentheses for the latter; when I remember to do so!).

>> Question about your comment about 'intermediate result sets' being
>> manipulated offline...  This sounds good in theory, and I'm sure it
works
>> most of the time, but I imagine it would not be a good thing if your 
>> select
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>> thinking in my "batch processing" mindset, so perhaps that's something
you
>> would simply never need to do.
>
>Certainly, the transactional approach would reduce the size of result 
>sets... :-)

:-)

Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

```
But the properties have already been declared in their corresponding
class(es).  Why should I declare them twice?!?  Of course this is where a
copybook might come in handy.  :-)

>A shame, because it looks
>> to be a nice feature.  Micro Focus allows you to use the "inline 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>I believe Fujitsu also supports it, although I don't use it myself. (Only
on 
>aesthetic grounds...it just doesn't look like COBOL to me, but I realise 
>that s a very empty objection :-))

Well, I often wish that COBOL didn't look quite as much like COBOL as it
does.  Many languages are criticized as being "write once/read never". 
Cobol, in my opinion, goes too far in the other direction.  Is easy to read,
but often a pain to write!  (Simply being too verbose).

In my real life Cobol life we tend to have things like...

01  AGN-A-GROUP-NAME.
    05  AGN-TYPE                   PIC X.
    05  AGN-BALANCE                PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  RPT2-IN-DTL1.
    05  RPT2-IN-DTL1-TYPE          PIC X.
    05  RPT2-IN-DTL1-BAL           PIC ZZZ,ZZZ,ZZ9.99+.

    MOVE AGN-TYPE TO RPT2-IN-DTL1-TYPE
    MOVE AGN-BALANCE TO RPT2-IN-DTL1-BAL

Conceivably we could instead do something like this:
01  GROUP-NAME.
    05  TYPE                       PIC X.
    05  BALANCE                    PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  RPT2-IN-DTL1.
    05  TYPE                       PIC X.
    05  BALANCE                    PIC ZZZ,ZZZ,ZZ9.99+.

    MOVE TYPE OF AGN-A-GROUP-NAME TO TYPE OF RPT2-IN-DTL1
    MOVE BALANCE OF AGN-A-GROUP-NAME TO BALANCE OF RPT2-IN-DTL1

But that is just not something that anyone here does.  (And yes, in this
case we could have even used MOVE CORR, but generally that probably wouldn't
work.)

I wouldn't say there's a shop standard that's against it.  Just that no one
uses it, and most likely no one sees any reason to use it.  I don't.  It's
too verbose.

Personally, I'd like to see something like this:

01  AGN-A-GROUP-NAME PREFIX AGN.
    05  TYPE                       PIC X.
    05  BALANCE                    PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  RPT2-IN-DTL1 PREFIX R2I-DTL1.
    05  TYPE                       PIC X.
    05  BAL                        PIC ZZZ,ZZZ,ZZ9.99+.

    MOVE AGN.TYPE TO R2I-DTL1.TYPE
    MOVE AGN.BALANCE TO R2I-DTL1.BAL

Maybe using ':' as the delimiter, instead of '.', since '.' has other
meanings.
    MOVE AGN:TYPE TO R2I-DTL1:TYPE
    MOVE AGN:BALANCE TO R2I-DTL1:BAL

Or maybe '::', since that's already in "standard COBOL"
    MOVE AGN::TYPE TO R2I-DTL1::TYPE
    MOVE AGN::BALANCE TO R2I-DTL1::BAL

And how about with TYPEDEFs?  I was looking forward to having TYPEDEFs until
I realized it would require me to use the 'OF' clause way too often.

How about:
01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
    05  ACCT-NBR                   PIC 9(10).
    05  TYPE                       PIC X.
    05  AMOUNT                     PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  PREV-TRAN                      TYPE TRANSACTION.
01  TRAN                           TYPE TRANSACTION.

IF TRAN.ACCT-NBR = PREV-TRAN.ACCT-NBR
    CONTINUE
ELSE
    PERFORM ACCT-BREAK
    MOVE TRAN TO PREV.TRAN
    MOVE ZERO TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT
END-IF
ADD TRAN.AMOUNT TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT

Just thoughts on what I might do if I were in charge of such things.  :-)

Frank


--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

```
"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message 
news:50fnlaF1esfutU1@mid.individual.net...
> Pete Dashwood<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> 01/05/07 8:41 PM >>>
>>
…[74 more quoted lines elided]…
> I just don't think that's going to happen.

What about using MF for development and deploying to the mainframe? MF have 
some great support tools in this area.
>
> Now if MF would port their compiler to VSE, that would be great!  :-)  But
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> copybook might come in handy.  :-)

I don't know about DotNET COBOL, but if a property is declared PUBLIC STATIC 
in a Class, you don't need todeclare it again anywhere. You don't even need 
to instantiate the Class it is defined in, in order to use it.

Quick C# example...

Form1 is a Class and has (among others) a Public Static property declared 
thus:

public partial class Form1 : Form
{
     private static string _DBname;
     // A read-write property that holds a DB path or ODBC system DSN after 
selection by the User
      public static string DBName
     {
      get
     {
      return _DBname;
     }
     set
     {
     _DBname = value;
     }
}

In another Class (Form2 - but it could be any Class...) I need to build a 
connection string for this DB. It is NOT defined anywhere in the current 
Class (Form2). I can create an Object Reference to it (for example - in 
fact, I can do anything I like with it...) as:

object objDBName = Form1.DBName;

The get method for the property is automatically invoked and the private 
_DBName variable form Form1 has its contents returned.

If I removed STATIC from the definition, I would then need an object 
reference pointing to an instance of  Form1 to be established before I could 
reference the property.

This is very useful and has saved my bacon on a few occasions while 
programming in C# :-)

I'm not sure what the equivalent would be in DotNET COBOL, but it would 
probably use the Repository. I'm not keen on using the repository because I 
only ever late bind when using OO COBOL, so I only make Repository entries 
when the program can't compile without them. Fujitsu NetCOBOL (which is an 
OO implemention but does not implement the DotNET Framework) allows 
prototyping of interfaces to be done and this might be another approach to 
the problem of not wanting thngs multiply defined.

>
>>A shame, because it looks
…[75 more quoted lines elided]…
>    MOVE AGN::BALANCE TO R2I-DTL1::BAL

I see little advantage in this, over what you originally coded :-)

>
> And how about with TYPEDEFs?  I was looking forward to having TYPEDEFs 
> until
> I realized it would require me to use the 'OF' clause way too often.
>

Yes, I agree in principle that COBOL could use SOME  kind of shorthand to 
replace 'OF' and 'IN' for qualification.

I suppose you could argue that OF and IN are only 2 characters (4 if you 
count spaces) and, in COBOL terms that shouldn't be onerous.

But I think they look ugly as well... :-)

> How about:
> 01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Hopefully you'd allow negated conditions to be used, instead of that awful 
CONTINUE ? :-)

Pete.
```

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:50gagoF1g0uboU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> I don't know about DotNET COBOL, but if a property is declared PUBLIC 
> STATIC in a Class, you don't need todeclare it again anywhere. You don't 
> even need to instantiate the Class it is defined in, in order to use it.

    Just as a heads up, different languages which target the .NET platform 
may assign different semantics or meaning to the same keyword. For example, 
in C#, the "static" keyword does what you claim it does, but in VB.NET, 
static does something completely different. To declare a field which exists 
at the class level, instead of at the instance level, you use the "Shared" 
keyword in VB.NET.

    In other words, the "public static" concept is a C# one (and Java uses a 
similar concept -- not sure if Cobol.NET uses a similar one), not a .NET 
concept.

    - Oliver
```

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-09T10:23:16-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50i1c7F1g015fU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <12oosqtmgaqcd81@news.supernews.com> <4v7ajgF1bf6u8U1@mid.individual.net> <1166980834.471149.102990@i12g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4v8qu1F1b75r0U1@mid.individual.net> <dElkh.4094$yx6.3439@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <4vf0t1F1bfj5nU1@mid.individual.net> <4vfl66F1as7a4U1@mid.individual.net> <4vgj6mF1a4g7nU1@mid.individual.net> <4vimr5F1cedo7U1@mid.individual.net> <4vj2kkF1co1akU1@mid.individual.net> <4vlbkiF1bf56sU1@mid.individual.net> <4vlnqgF1c247oU1@mid.individual.net> <4vm6ldF16ggd7U1@mid.individual.net> <4vvj6rF1dqm00U1@mid.individual.net> <500b4bF1d47t2U1@mid.individual.net> <enl1cs$4lg$1@nntp.fujitsu-siemens.com> <506ruoF1ehb1uU1@mid.individual.net> <507nhmF1f24rfU1@mid.individual.net> <5085fhF1equqlU1@mid.individual.net> <508cr5F1ekph4U1@mid.individual.net> <508k3qF1e9pibU1@mid.individual.net> <50fnlaF1esfutU1@mid.individual.net> <50gagoF1g0uboU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> 01/08/07 6:47 PM >>>

Frank Swarbrick

>>>> I considered using properties instead of methods, but the requirement
to
>>>> declare them in the repository makes that, umm, inconvenient,
especially
>>>> when there are dozens or even hundreds of them.
>>>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> class(es).  Why should I declare them twice?!?  Of course this is where
a
>> copybook might come in handy.  :-)
>
>I don't know about DotNET COBOL, but if a property is declared PUBLIC
STATIC 
>in a Class, you don't need todeclare it again anywhere. You don't even need

>to instantiate the Class it is defined in, in order to use it.
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>     // A read-write property that holds a DB path or ODBC system DSN after

>selection by the User
>      public static string DBName
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>reference pointing to an instance of  Form1 to be established before I
could 
>reference the property.
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>probably use the Repository. I'm not keen on using the repository because I

>only ever late bind when using OO COBOL, so I only make Repository entries

>when the program can't compile without them. Fujitsu NetCOBOL (which is an

>OO implemention but does not implement the DotNET Framework) allows 
>prototyping of interfaces to be done and this might be another approach to

>the problem of not wanting thngs multiply defined.

I'm not sure we are on the same page here.  Take the following...

public class Class1
{
    private int myInt;
    public int MyString
    {
        get
        {
            return myInt;
        }
        set
        {
            myInt = value
        }
}

public class MainClass
{
    public static void Main( string[] args )
    {
        Class1 myClass = new Class1;
        myClass.MyInt = 25;
        System.Console.WriteLine(myClass.MyInt);
    }
}

In "standard" OO COBOL you might have something like:

class-id. Class1 as "Class1".
    object.
        data division.
        working-storage section.
        77  my-int    binary-long property.
    end object.
end class Class1.

program-id. Main.
repository.
    class Class1 as "Class1"
    property my-int
    .
data division.
working-storage section.
77  myClass object reference Class1.
procedure division.
    set myClass to Class1::"New"()
    move 25 to my-int of myClass
    display my-int of myClass
    exit program.
end program Main.

I had to define my-int in Class1, and then declare it in the repository of
Main, even though I declared the entire class Class1 in Main.  I'm not sure
why there is a need to declare properties in the repository.  Seems to me
the compiler should be able to read the external repository and see that
my-int is a property of myClass.

Anyway, MF COBOL does allow this if you use the property in a manner similar
to the inline invocation, eg:

program-id. Main.
repository.
    class Class1 as "Class1"
    .
data division.
working-storage section.
77  myClass object reference Class1.
procedure division.
    set myClass to Class1::"New"()
    move 25 to myClass::"my-int"
    display myClass::"my-int"
    exit program.
end program Main.

Doing it this way there is no need to have the 'property my-int' declaration
in the repository.  Seems like a small thing in this example, since there's
only one property, but imagine 100 properties!

I'm guessing the reason has something to do with my-int being a data-name in
the first example and a literal in the second.  Seems an odd distincition,
but I am not a compiler writer, so there may be some justification I don't
know about.  Still doesn't make me like it any more, though!  :-)

>> In my real life Cobol life we tend to have things like...
>>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>> uses it, and most likely no one sees any reason to use it.  I don't. 
It's
>> too verbose.
>>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>I see little advantage in this, over what you originally coded :-)


The point, as little as it may be, is that the sub-level fields (05, etc.)
need not be declared with the entire name.  IE, TYPE vs AGN-TYPE.  (I just
realized that TYPE is a reserved word, but...)  A small thing?  Certainly. 
But I would find it nice.  After all, I'm only dreaming here.

>> And how about with TYPEDEFs?  I was looking forward to having TYPEDEFs 
>> until
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>But I think they look ugly as well... :-)

It's ugly and, to me, a bit backwards.  Current: AGN-BALANCE.  With typedef:
BALANCE OF AGN.  Backwards!  :-)  My way: AGN.BALANCE.  Better!  :-)

>> How about:
>> 01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>> Just thoughts on what I might do if I were in charge of such things. 
:-)
>>
>
>Hopefully you'd allow negated conditions to be used, instead of that awful

>CONTINUE ? :-)

Sure, why not.  :-)  I've had it beaten into me to avoid negated conditions.
 But if they are simple enough (such as in this case) I'm not really against
them.

Frank
```

```

"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message
news:50fnlaF1esfutU1@mid.individual.net...
[snip]
> And how about with TYPEDEFs?  I was looking forward to having TYPEDEFs
until
> I realized it would require me to use the 'OF' clause way too often.

It may be possible to avoid using OF (or IN) by using
the 66 RENAMES.

> How about:
> 01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
>     05  ACCT-NBR                   PIC 9(10).
>     05  TYPE                       PIC X.
Change to TYP since TYPE is a reserved word.
>     05  AMOUNT                     PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.
>
> 01  PREV-TRAN                      TYPE TRANSACTION.
        66 PREV-TRAN-ACCT-NBR RENAMES ACCT-NBR.
        66 PREV-TRAN-TYP RENAMES TYP.
        66 PREV-TRAN-AMOUNT RENAMES AMOUNT.
> 01  TRAN                           TYPE TRANSACTION.
        66 TRAN-ACCT-NBR RENAMES ACCT-NBR.
        66 TRAN-TYP RENAMES TYP.
        66 TRAN-AMOUNT RENAMES AMOUNT.

Replace the "." separator with "-" in the following.

> IF TRAN.ACCT-NBR = PREV-TRAN.ACCT-NBR
>     CONTINUE
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Just thoughts on what I might do if I were in charge of such things.  :-)

However 66 RENAMES can not be used with entries
subordinate to an OCCURS clause or an 88 condition-name.

TYPEDEFs may be used to maintain consistent USAGE
and PICTURE, yet, with 66 RENAMES, allow most
procedure division statements to avoid the use of OF or IN.

[I checked the above statements using Micro Focus 3.2.24
(where TYPEDEF is an extension) and by reading the FDIS
for COBOL 2002.]
```

```
"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message
news:50ki2hF1g4q80U1@mid.individual.net...
> >
> >
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> Even more typing!  Thanks, but no.  :-)

I do not understand!

TYPEDEF saves several hundred (maybe a few thousand)
characters. OF (or IN) adds a few hundred to several hundred
characters. Judicious use of RENAMES adds a few hundred;
but saves a few hundred to several hundred characters by
avoiding OF (or IN). [Depending on the program.]

The key, it seems, is that, where the judicious use of RENAMES
adds fewer characters than are saved by using TYPEDEF, the
result will be less typing. Therefore, I do not understand how
less typing would be "Even more typing!".

Perhaps you could explain your comment.
```

```
Rick Smith<ricksmith@mfi.net> 01/10/07 12:45 PM >>>
>
>"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >> And how about with TYPEDEFs?  I was looking forward to having
TYPEDEFs
>> >until
>> >> I realized it would require me to use the 'OF' clause way too often.
…[60 more quoted lines elided]…
>Perhaps you could explain your comment.

I think we may be on two different pages.  I was comparing yours:

01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
    05  ACCT-NBR                   PIC 9(10).
    05  TYP                        PIC X.
    05  AMOUNT                     PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  PREV-TRAN                      TYPE TRANSACTION.
    66 PREV-TRAN-ACCT-NBR RENAMES ACCT-NBR.
    66 PREV-TRAN-TYP RENAMES TYP.
    66 PREV-TRAN-AMOUNT RENAMES AMOUNT.

01  TRAN                           TYPE TRANSACTION.
    66 TRAN-ACCT-NBR RENAMES ACCT-NBR.
    66 TRAN-TYP RENAMES TYP.
    66 TRAN-AMOUNT RENAMES AMOUNT.

IF TRAN-ACCT-NBR = PREV-TRAN-ACCT-NBR
    CONTINUE
ELSE
    PERFORM ACCT-BREAK
    MOVE TRAN TO PREV-TRAN
    MOVE ZERO TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT
END-IF
ADD TRAN-AMOUNT TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT

With my "pseudo-COBOL" version.

01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
    05  ACCT-NBR                   PIC 9(10).
    05  TYP                        PIC X.
    05  AMOUNT                     PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  PREV-TRAN                      TYPE TRANSACTION.
01  TRAN                           TYPE TRANSACTION.

IF TRAN.ACCT-NBR = PREV-TRAN.ACCT-NBR
    CONTINUE
ELSE
    PERFORM ACCT-BREAK
    MOVE TRAN TO PREV.TRAN
    MOVE ZERO TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT
END-IF
ADD TRAN.AMOUNT TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT

Whereas I think you must have been comparing yours versus a version not
using TYPEDEF.

Or were you comparing it to this?
01  TRANSACTION TYPEDEF.
    05  ACCT-NBR                   PIC 9(10).
    05  TYP                        PIC X.
    05  AMOUNT                     PIC S9(9)V99 COMP-3.

01  PREV-TRAN                      TYPE TRANSACTION.
01  TRAN                           TYPE TRANSACTION.

IF ACCT-NBR OF TRAN = ACCT-NBR OF PREV-TRAN
    CONTINUE
ELSE
    PERFORM ACCT-BREAK
    MOVE TRAN TO PREV-TRAN
    MOVE ZERO TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT
END-IF
ADD AMOUNT OF TRAN TO TOTAL-TRAN-AMOUNT

Even that version is less typing than your version.

What I was saying is that, you are typing "ALT-FIELD-NAME RENAMES
FIELD-NAME" in order to be able to say "MOVE ALT-FIELD-NAME TO ..." instead
of "MOVE FIELD-NAME OF XXX TO...".  It looks like more typing to me.

I'm probably just not understanding what you are getting at.

Frank




--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

```
"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message
news:50l8a0F1gbnbdU1@mid.individual.net...

> >
…[144 more quoted lines elided]…
> FIELD-NAME" in order to be able to say "MOVE ALT-FIELD-NAME TO ..."
instead
> of "MOVE FIELD-NAME OF XXX TO...".  It looks like more typing to me.
>
> I'm probably just not understanding what you are getting at.

Assuming TYPEDEF is used, then with regard to typing,
the total number of characters that will be used depends
upon the frequency of reference in a complete program;
not in code used as an example. If it is correctly anticipated
that qualification will be used frequently with particular
data-names and those data-names are renamed, there
will be less typing. I hope that is clear. <g>
```

```
TYPEDEFs ...
   Just wanted to make certain that those thinking about using the ISO 2002 
enhancement of "TYPEDEFs" also look at the implications of "strong" and "weak" 
typing in that Standard.   I haven't checked it, but I *think* that SAME AS in 
the '02 Standard does NOT introduce those restrictions or advantages (depending 
upon your view of STRONG typing).
```

```

"Karl Kiesel" <Karl.Kiesel@fujitsu-siemens.com> wrote in message
news:enl1cs$4lg$1@nntp.fujitsu-siemens.com...
>
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> schrieb im
Newsbeitrag
> news:500b4bF1d47t2U1@mid.individual.net...
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> Any pro and con arguments would be welcome.

I find the REPLACE statement in COBOL 85 to be
too limiting since only one REPLACE statement may
be in effect at one time. [This has been corrected in
the 2002 COBOL standard.] Because I use a COBOL
85 system, I tend to use extensions to overcome those
limitations. However, with COBOL 85 conforming
code, there may be a more interesting approach.

If ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT is used only as a sending
field (it is, in the program presented), then the following
may be preferred.

Insert, at the beginning of working-storage, ...

REPLACE ==ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT== BY ==40==.

Change, ...

000059     12 NAME-VALUE-PAIR OCCURS 40
000060                        INDEXED BY ARGS-X1.

To, ...

000059     12 NAME-VALUE-PAIR OCCURS ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT
000060                        INDEXED BY ARGS-X1.

Remove the statement and comment, ...

000069 01  ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT   PIC S9(4) COMP VALUE 40.
000070*> MUST MATCH VALUE IN OCCURS ABOVE.

In the procedure division, the statement, ...

000587     MOVE ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT TO MAX-ARGUMENTS

After the REPLACE statement, will become, ...

000587     MOVE 40 TO MAX-ARGUMENTS

When moving to COBOL 2002, the above REPLACE
statement may be changed to, ...

01 ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT CONSTANT 40.

Alternatively, one may simply force the correct value,
at the beginning of the program, with the statement, ...

COMPUTE ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT =
    FUNCTION LENGTH (ARGUMENTS)
        / FUNCTION LENGTH (NAME-VALUE-PAIR)

Or, similarly, test the value and issue an error message
if it is not correct.
```

```
Yes, but a REPLACE statement can have multiple replaces within it.  Here's
some production code as an example:

REPLACE
   ==:PCB-CNT:== BY ==2==  
   ==:CNA:== BY ==GAI-CNA==
   ==:NPP:== BY ==3==
   ==:LPN:== BY ==22==
   ==:CTR-CNT:== BY ==39==
.

     05  NOTICES-PER-PAGE        PIC S9(3) COMP-3 VALUE :NPP:.

 01  NOTICE-LINE-TABLE.                             
     05                          OCCURS :NPP: TIMES.
         10  NOTICE-LINE         OCCURS :LPN: TIMES.
             15  NL-CC           PIC X.             
             15  NL-LINE         PIC X(79).         

 01  PCB-PTR-TBL.                                      
     05  PCB-PTR                 OCCURS :PCB-CNT: TIMES
                                 POINTER.              


         SET CTR-IDX TO :CTR-CNT:

     SET CTR-MAX TO :CTR-CNT:


>If ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT is used only as a sending
>field (it is, in the program presented), then the following
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>000587     MOVE 40 TO MAX-ARGUMENTS

True enough!

>When moving to COBOL 2002, the above REPLACE
>statement may be changed to, ...
>
>01 ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT CONSTANT 40.

Cool.

>Alternatively, one may simply force the correct value,
>at the beginning of the program, with the statement, ...
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>        / FUNCTION LENGTH (NAME-VALUE-PAIR)

This won't allow you to use ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT in the occurs clause.

>Or, similarly, test the value and issue an error message
>if it is not correct.

Do you mean something like:
IF ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT NOT EQUAL TO (FUNCTION LENGTH(ARGUMENTS) / FUNCTION
LENGTH(NAME-VALUE-PAIR)
    ...do error message here...
END-IF
?

I don't think this because it always gives a compiler warning that the
statement will never be executed.  (I don't like superfulous warnings, if I
can avoid them.)

Frank
```

```
... NAME-VALUE-PAIR))

           IF ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT NOT EQUAL TO
               (FUNCTION LENGTH (ARGUMENTS)
                   / FUNCTION LENGTH (NAME-VALUE-PAIR))
               DISPLAY QUOTE "ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT" QUOTE
                   SPACE "HAS INCORRECT VALUE"
           END-IF

I entered the above code into the program and changed
the VALUE clause for ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT to +0 to
make certain it would be incorrect. After checking and
running the code, the following message appeared on
the display.

"ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT" HAS INCORRECT VALUE

I then restored the value 40, checked, and ran the program.
No message was displayed.

I am certain that the comparison works correctly, on a
conforming compiler, with no warnings or other errors.
```

```

"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message
news:508bh2F1eu11iU1@mid.individual.net...
[snip]
> I did have to add a subscript to NAME-VALUE-PAIR to get this to work.  Did
> you define your table in some other way that makes this unnecessary?
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> :-)

I reduced to program NVPAMS to its minimum and
renamed it as included here.

       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID.     NVTEST.
       DATA DIVISION.
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01  ARGUMENTS.
           12 NAME-VALUE-PAIR OCCURS 40
                        INDEXED BY ARGS-X1.
               15 ARG-NAME    PIC X(25).
               15 ARG-VALUE   PIC X(25).
               15 ARG-EOG     PIC X.  *> INDICATES THAT THIS ENTRY IS
                               *> THE END OF A "GROUP" OF
                               *> PARAMETERS.

                   88 ARG-END-OF-GROUP  VALUE "1".

       01  ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT   PIC S9(4) COMP VALUE +0.
       *> MUST MATCH VALUE IN OCCURS ABOVE.

       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       MAIN SECTION.
       000.
           IF ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT NOT EQUAL TO
               (FUNCTION LENGTH (ARGUMENTS)
                   / FUNCTION LENGTH (NAME-VALUE-PAIR))
               DISPLAY QUOTE "ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT" QUOTE
                   SPACE "HAS INCORRECT VALUE"
           END-IF
           STOP RUN.

The compiler I used was Micro Focus COBOL 3.2.24.
It works as shown and with the subscripted
NAME-VALUE-PAIR(1).

I see nothing in the LRM, LENGTH intrinsic function
syntax and general rules, to suggest that only one of
these is correct.
```

```
Karl,
  Interesting approach.  However, as someone else commented, the '85 Standard 
REPLACE statement had its limitation (only one "active" REPLACE at a time; 
although it could impact many different text words).

As was also indicated, this gets "fixed" in the '02 Standard (and I know you all 
have implemented parts of that).  Have you implemented the REPLACE ALSO 
"enhancements"?  It would seem to me, that the '02 Standard CONSTANT would also 
"solve" this type of application issue.  Therefore, if you (or anyone <G>) had a 
fully '02 compliant compiler, then I think THIS would be a time for CONSTANT 
rather than REPLACE - but that's just a "style" issue.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag 
news:4ptnh.421381$X52.377396@fe03.news.easynews.com...
> Karl,
>  Interesting approach.  However, as someone else commented, the '85 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> a time for CONSTANT rather than REPLACE - but that's just a "style" issue.

Bill,
we have neither implemented the REPLACE ALSO, nor the constants;
that was the background for my question, to get some idea, which of these 
features could be more beneficial for a user.
Besides, I have an uneasy feeling with respect to constants: though we did 
not check it thorougly, it looks as if a compiler would have to do a 
complete syntactic analysis to find these constant definitions, but for such 
an analysis, the values defined by the constants would already be needed - 
seems to be some cyclic dependency!

regards
Karl Kiesel
Fujitsu Siemens Computers
```

```
>Bill,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Besides, I have an uneasy feeling with respect to constants: though we did

>not check it thorougly, it looks as if a compiler would have to do a 
>complete syntactic analysis to find these constant definitions, but for
such 
>an analysis, the values defined by the constants would already be needed -

>seems to be some cyclic dependency!

I am of the opinion that CONSTANT declaration is preferable to the REPLACE.

Do you implement the "78 level" extension?  I imagine that

78 MYCONST VALUE 25.

and

01  MYCONST CONSTANT 25.

Would work in pretty much the same manner.  

I know that OpenCobol implements the 78-level constant, and it does it in
the "scanner" code.  I believe this means that by the time it gets to the
parser the 78-level constant has already been converted to it's
corresponding literal.  I imagine that the ISO2002 CONSTANT 01 level could
be implemented fairly simply using similar code.

Check out
http://open-cobol.cvs.sourceforge.net/open-cobol/open-cobol/cobc/scanner.l?v
iew=markup if I'm not making any sense...

By the way, can I get you to port you COBOL to the z/VSE operating system. 
:-)
Seriously, I love that you guys are looking to implement so much of the 2002
COBOL standard.  Wish my vendor cared as much.

Frank


--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA
```

```
I have done this upon occasion, although just slightly different:

     REPLACE  ==:a-t-l:== BY ==40==.
     ....
     ....
         12 NAME-VALUE-PAIR OCCURS :a-t-l:
     ....
     01  ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT  PIC S9(4) COMP VALUE :a-t-l:.

78  ARGS-TABLE-LIMIT 40.
```

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> FYI,
>   The current Micro Focus product for .NET development comes with both the 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
I have been browsing the security problems involved with mixing 
components and languages. C# and .NET are better than others for bounds 
checking, string length, and stack problems we see in the news today.
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buffer_overflow

A paper from SANS has a little more depth, and background, as well as 
how it's done. In the 24 pages it explains quite a lot, and is 
supprisingly easy to read.
http://www.sans.org/reading_room/whitepapers/securecode/386.php

COBOL is not mentioned in either of the two links above, but the methods 
suggested to block a lot of the errors involve replacing the libraries 
with other safer ones, and third-party products that add the protections 
simply provided by COBOL. By design, COBOL doesn't lend itself to most 
of the above, but some protections could be disabled for fasted 
processing. I wouldn't do that when mixing languages. Over half of the 
vulnerabilities reported by SANS are failures to validate the 
(user's/caller's) input, which I think was taught in my 3rd day of COBOL 
class.

Just my $.02

Gary
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> But despite all of that, it is quite obvious that my productivity in Visual 
> Studio 2005 is MUCH better than my productivity using the Fujitsu IDE.

This impressed me when I first started working with VB6 many years ago. 
  What I found especially useful was that it syntax-checked each line as 
you typed it.  I wrote a VB program to parse our DMS schema and compute 
card columns.  I looked at it a few years ago, when I already had some 
years of VB experience under my belt - it was horrendous!  But, it was 
syntactically correct.

A little over a year ago (or has it been 2 now - wow!), I used it to 
strip the data items and create relational tables.  Now, the code is 
much tighter - and to calculate the total size of an OCCURS clause, I 
just read ahead in the dataset to get all the PIC sizes, and multiply by 
the number of times.

> This made me think about tools.
> 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> productivity improvements.

SlickEdit (formerly "Visual SlickEdit") does many of these things for 
COBOL, although not to the extent that VS2005 does for C#.  Our 
productivity shot through the roof when we began using it for our COBOL 
code development.  It will pop up a list of variable defined in working 
storage, has context-sensitive help, and even has a window that will 
show you the definition of whatever variable the cursor is on.  (All 
this assumes you have the thing configured correctly - it will build 
"tag lists" for the COBOL COPY libraries, for example, but you have to 
do that for the feature to work.)

It's a little pricey, IMO, for an editor ($200 last I checked), but of 
course, the US taxpayers provide us our copies.  :)

Back in 2002, I was out at Oracle in Silicon Valley, at a week-long 
"hey, pick us!" pitch they were giving the USAF.  Some of the folks 
there were from the Software Engineering Institute (SEI) at 
Carnegie-Mellon University.  One of the ladies there saw it open on my 
laptop and said "Hey, is that Visual SlickEdit?"  I told her that it 
was, and she said, "we use that all the time for our Java!  I never 
would have thought to use it for COBOL."  I thought that was kind of 
funny because, at the time, I had never thought of using it for Java.

> But.most importantly of all, the environment is NOT focused on code 
> development. It is pure event driven programming. You DON'T start by writing 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> when events occur in it.

I don't really have a good comparison here, because our COBOL is based 
on the Unisys mainframe.  I do know that there are tools that really 
integrate Windows with it (such as the ability to map drives to 
mainframe program files, interactive debug execute on the mainframe from 
the PC).  Of course, none of them have been approved for use.  (Don't 
get me started on that...)

> So, it looks like I will be winding down my COBOL development.
> 
> The tools for cutting edge development are MUCH better and cheaper than 
> COBOL. It remains useful for batch development, which is what it was 
> invented for, and where we came in...

And what it's still used for in our environment.  We're seeing thousands 
of batch jobs run per day - in fact, we recently had to change the way 
we handled these runs, because the users were literally bringing the 
system to its knees.  (Have you ever seen a mainframe beg?  It ain't 
pretty...)  Now, we have a controller, and we're actually clearing more 
runs than we did with the old way, because we're controlling the number 
that are started.

That's the one thing that I'm not sure about - there still is a need for 
innovation within the COBOL community.  There are processes that are 
crucial for businesses and governments that cannot be replaced 
immediately, but could be improved.  What happens to these apps as the 
IT world "moves on"?  I don't see improvements - in our arena, every 
system we interface with has had big problems with a fraction of the 
transaction volume that we clear on a slow day.  There was one recently 
that went to 30-plus second response times when they got 4 users!  Then, 
you deal with all these policies and things that you have to deal with 
to be "compliant," and they all seem to suck bandwidth and performance. 
  Sure, if you're running on a huge box, it'll be passable - but are the 
network folks giving us funds to upgrade our hardware?  Heh.

For good or bad, the above won't be my concern for the system on which 
I've worked for the past 9 years.  In a way, I hate that, because I have 
so many other ideas that I know would improve the system with minimal 
investment of code.  I guess I just have trouble detaching myself from 
this thing that I've worked so hard on for the past 9 years.

Probably more than you wanted to hear...  :)  Those are some of the 
thoughts I've had about productivity.
```

```
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

It's funny (and interesting) how we are blinkered by what we know...:-)

>> But.most importantly of all, the environment is NOT focused on code 
>> development. It is pure event driven programming. You DON'T start by 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>> what you want to happen when events occur in it.

This follows from the component based approach. Thinking in "functions" (or 
"production line machines"), encapsulated pieces of functionality that are 
stable and robust, is a really good way to develop applications. We get too 
tied into code. Need to get data and start thinking about READ...AT 
END...NOT AT END... etc. when we should be thinking about WHAT we are 
getting, WHY we are getting it at this point, and at how many different 
places we are going to need to get it, and providing a function that simply 
gets it.

I believe the difference between procedural and non-procedural approaches 
underlies this. COBOL programmers are used to a sequential procedural 
approach with rigid control and event driven programs aren't like that. 
Objects lend themselves to being used anywhere at anytime so design can be 
much more flexible.

>
> I don't really have a good comparison here, because our COBOL is based on 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes, COBOL is excellent for sequential batch processing and will fill that 
requirement for some time to come. Eventually, transactions will be 
processed so fast that Batch will not be necessary, even reports will be 
spooled online as transactions occur, and so there will be less dependence 
on Batch. Only historical reporting would need a batch approach. But that 
time is still a few years off yet.

> That's the one thing that I'm not sure about - there still is a need for 
> innovation within the COBOL community.  There are processes that are 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> folks giving us funds to upgrade our hardware?  Heh.

A very good point. I suspect that rather than fix or improve, these systems 
will simply be allowed to atrophy until they are replaced. If the 
replacements, as you describe above, are far worse than the existing systems 
then they simply should not be accepted until proper SLAs are agreed and put 
in place.
>
> For good or bad, the above won't be my concern for the system on which 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> this thing that I've worked so hard on for the past 9 years.

If you didn't care about it you wouldn't be much good at it :-)

We all get emotionally attached to things that have been a big part of our 
working life, even though we know we shouldn't.

why don't you write a paper on the system, outling some of the areas that 
could be improved , and suggesting some approaches so that anyone taking 
over at least has the option, based on your years of experience, to 
implement some of your ideas?

Even if they don't, at least you know you gave it your best shot.

>
> Probably more than you wanted to hear...  :)

Not at all. I for one, found your observations interesting.

>Those are some of the thoughts I've had about productivity.

It's something that many of us should give some thought to...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-12-30T12:38:26+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4vln7jF1cjp07U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <4vlc76F1bf56sU2@mid.individual.net>`

```

"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message 
news:4vlc76F1bf56sU2@mid.individual.net...
<snip>
> What about the manager who wants to know the total amount of transactions
> processed today?  I guess there would be something that uses SQL to do 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the realm of my experience, and thus fraught with many unknowns.

It needs a slight change of concept.

A batch process is an accumulation of events that happened in the past. An 
on-line process involves events happening now. (Usually in the form of 
transactions)

Therefore, theoretically at least, a batch process can be removed if the 
transactions happening "now" make provision for the things the Batch process 
would have been run to find out. (Sorry, I know that sounds clumsy, but it 
is the best I can do... :-))

Another way to look at it is that the batch process emulates a series of 
events over time. Therefore, if those events provided what the batch process 
is going to, as they happened, there would be no need for the batch process.

Because of the fundamental laws of arithmetic you can achieve the same 
result with a series of individual accumulations as they occur, as you can 
with a bunch of retrospective accumulations.

I have worked for a few major banks and I know of one that does online 
accrual and interest calculation. (It's in London and is not a  retail 
bank). Interest on any account is available as it stands "now" without 
running a batch process. Transactions affecting the balance automatically 
post to the interest account so it is always up to date. Interest is 
calculated on the days between the old and new balance at the time any 
transaction on the account is run, and is accrued. (A transaction is run to 
get the current interest and it does exactly the same calculation. The old 
and new balance will be the same, but the dates between them will require 
interest to be calculated to bring the interest up to date.) This stuff is 
complex because of interest rate fluctuations (rates are held as a 
"collection" ordered by date), but it can certainly be done.

Statements can also be built up on line with lines being added as 
transactions occur. A "virtual image" of the statement is held until it is 
requested or otherwise triggered by a statement date. My local bank here in 
NZ offers online statements and I no longer get paper ones. I can request a 
statement any time I want and it will reflect the state of my account as it 
is "now" or between whatever dates I specify (up to about one year ago). The 
information is presented immediately on request, via a Web Service, and 
interest is calculated and shown for the period I requested the statement. 
(Along with fees as you mentioned.)

Howard is right in that Data Warehousing can simply remove much of the need 
for batch processing, with data cubes already providing consolidated and 
accumulated pictures which are updated in real time, along with the 
capability to drill down to detail right now, as the transaction runs.

Databases that have stored procedures triggered by transactions can fully 
automate much of what would have required a batch process to extract.

I think batch processing will be with us for some time to come (mainly 
because of what Michael observed: "That's the way we've always done it"), 
and not because of any real need for it. As the new approaches and tools 
become more firmly established, and the demand for information right "now" 
increases, we will see diminishng dependency on it.

Pete.
```

```
Just my two cents - Data Warehousing often requires a really staggering 
amount of data manipulation and reduction before it is stored in the 
warehouse. COBOL, in particular BATCH COBOL, is really good for this.

Secondly, OLTP systems usually have a batch processing back end to them 
somewhere or another. BATCH processing is just cheaper and faster, and as 
such will stick around for a long time to come. ;)  COBOL is again, good for 
that.

-Paul
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T01:00:29+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga>`

```

"P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
news:pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga...
> Just my two cents - Data Warehousing often requires a really staggering 
> amount of data manipulation and reduction before it is stored in the 
> warehouse. COBOL, in particular BATCH COBOL, is really good for this.

As experience is gained, Data Warehouses become better. Designs improve, 
processing is faster, facilities are more diverse. I know one company where 
NO COBOL has ever been used, and yet their Data Warehouse provides 
incredible support to their business. In particular, cubes can be updated 
from online transactions and automatically consolidated by the warehousing 
software. No batch processing is involved, as far as I know.
>
> Secondly, OLTP systems usually have a batch processing back end to them 
> somewhere or another.

Only because that is how it has "always been done"... :-) (See Michael's 
comment elsewhere).

I addressed this in my response to Frank, in this thread, on 30th December. 
Smarter transactions, running on more powerful processors can obviate batch 
processing. DW software facilitates this.

A properly implemented DW (and I think this may have been Howard's point) 
can obviate a good deal, if not all, of batch processing.


>BATCH processing is just cheaper and faster, and as such will stick around 
>for a long time to come. ;)

I believe it will, but NOT for the reasons you state. It ISN'T cheaper 
(compared to an on-line transaction that sets up accrual in real time) and 
it certainly isn't faster. Adding online accrual to a transaction, for 
example, means it only accesses "today's" data; a batch process would have 
to look at EVERY day's data. You might thik that processing cycles consumed 
should be the same, but it ain't necessarily so...

(Consider an online transaction that calculates interest on an account which 
last had a balance change 3 weeks ago. The transaction can access a 
collection of interest rates for the dates between then and today and 
calculate the accrued interest; it only has to calculate for one period (the 
time since the last balance chage). It "knows" the balance changed because 
it is the transaction that changed it, and it wouldn't be running UNLESS the 
balance changed...

A batch process doing the same job uses a "brute force" approach, looking at 
every account every night, whether the balance changed or not, because it 
can't know whether the balance changed until it looks at it... :-) Total 
processing cycles consumed (especially EXCPs, if you work in an IBM 
environment) will be (much) higher for the batch process.)

This, admittedly contrived, example demonstrates quite clearly the 
fundamental difference between event driven (read, "on-line") and 
non-event-driven (read, "batch") processing. OO lends itself to event driven 
processing; procedural programming doesn't. That is why OO is taking over 
the world... (whether the people using it understand this or not... all they 
know is that Objects are flexible and responsive and designed to handle 
events; procedural code simply isn't...)

Talk to your Ops Manager about the effort required to get everything 
processed through the overnight "Batch window"... You can see Ops people age 
every time a new system with Batch processing is released...The usual 
solution is to throw more hardware at it, but then there is all the 
paperwork and hassle of getting approvals... Politically, it is much easier 
to get approval when online transactions have slowed, because they are so 
much more visible.

I think Batch will be with us for a few years yet, as I stated before, but 
it won't be because it CAN'T be decommissioned; rather, it will be because 
that is what is in place, and it will take a while for the DWs and networks 
to achieve the power that can obviate it, and for managers to wake up to 
that fact.

>COBOL is again, good for that.

Yes, it is. Outstanding, in fact.

And canvas was superb for driving square-rigged sailing vessels.

But you won't find a lot of it on a container ship.

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote

> fundamental difference between event driven (read, "on-line") and
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

While I can believe that COBOl will eventually be retired, I don't
believe batch processing will ever completely go away.  Events such as
are invoked by as tax authorities wanting yearly company accounts and
tax computations are probably too much to suddenly throw at an on-line
system, there would probably be severe degradation of service, even if
many of the underlying figures had already been calculated.  Events
such as monthly/weekly payrolls, cheque printing, etc also are probably
best done in a batch process.

I would also add that while the "we have always done it that way"
factor can be an obstacle, one also has to consider whether it is worth
the effort in making the change unless there is a perceived benefit in
changing something that already works, and who is going to provide the
budget and manpower to implement a new system.

Robert
```

```

"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
news:ce747$4597ebb6$454920f8$18039@KNOLOGY.NET...
> Robert Jones wrote:
>> message snipped
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Sorry, I responded to Robert before I read your post, Daniel.

I agree that payroll is a very good example of a process that is handled 
well by batch  processing, but I also don't think it HAS to be.

I remember many years ago working on a payroll system for Auckland Hospital. 
The design took nearly two years (I didn't do it - I was a programmer). It 
had to cater for every conceivable occupation from janitors to senior 
surgeons, all with their own special awards, penalty rates, holidays, 
payment methods, and payment periods. Just getting information into it (who 
was on leave, who had been  promoted, who needed a final pay because they 
were leaving, how many hours were worked by people who were paid hourly, 
etc. ad nauseum) kept the Payroll department fully employed just entering 
transactions to notify it of exceptions to the underlying base.

If you want a vision here is some holiday induced fantasy... ;-)

Nowadays, I believe all of the base processing could be achieved by stored 
procedures. These procedures could be triggered against each employee 
account on a staggered schedule (say, one every minute, or whatever, 
depending on the number of employees.  - the hospital had around 700, so, if 
you allowed 1 day at the start of the month (say the first banking day of 
the month)  you would do a base calculation for an employee once every two 
minutes. (Hardly a noticeable load on the transaction system.) The result of 
this would be a schedule of payments to go to the Bank on Payday, with a 
line for each employee, keyed on their employee id. During the month, 
transactions for exceptions would occur and each of these would modify the 
line on the schedule for the affected employee, as well as updating their 
account, tax, deductions etc. Come Payday, the schedule is already prepared 
and reflects up to the minute transactions and events. It is sent to the 
Bank, everyone gets their money... Hooray!

Obviously, this is simplistic and assumes that everyone is paid on the same 
day and in the same periodicity, and by direct credit, but it could be 
modified for exceptions to this. For example the schedule could have an 
indicator for the payment method, and trigger a cheque instead of a 
transfer. Separate schedules could be prepared for people who are paid 
weekly, monthly, hourly, whatever. It is feasible and it doesn't require 
daily batch runs or even monthly batch runs with the consequent impact on 
batch windows.

Will it happen? Extremely unlikely, but it could... A small business with a 
small number (say, 50) of employees might find this a very useful way to 
"automate payroll and put it on a back burner".. Because it is on-line it 
would reflect commission payments into the period they were earned (very 
popular with the salesmen), rather than waiting until next month because the 
batch cut-off was missed.

Having said all of the above, I have to agree that payroll is one example of 
something that is best addressed (at least right now), by a batch process.

Pete.
```

```
Payroll IS a batch process.

can be a day.. and I guess - in theory anyway - it could be as little as an 
hour; and of course for piecework it could be a piece.

When the underlying business process is itself a batch process, seems to me 
the data processing for same should also be a batch process.
```

- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net> <1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <ce747$4597ebb6$454920f8$18039@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vr64cF1cutp8U1@mid.individual.net> <HS8mh.10367$yC5.3200@newssvr27.news.prodigy.net> <4vt9r2F1dd0ocU1@mid.individual.net> <Dvtmh.28392$hI.3059@newssvr11.news.prodigy.net>`

```

"Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote in message 
news:Dvtmh.28392$hI.3059@newssvr11.news.prodigy.net...
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
> news:4vt9r2F1dd0ocU1@mid.individual.net...
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> between date-time 'A' and date-time 'B'

An online transaction does that, where values of A and B = 'now'.

As history is simply a collection of 'now' moments, batch processing can be 
obviated by extending the scope of the transaction to do accrual. 
(Previously, we did as little as possible in online transactions to try and 
keep them quick, backing them up with batch processing to pick up what they 
didn't do... My suggestion is, that with techniques like stored database 
procedures that can be triggered to run outside of the actual transaction, 
and running on much mre powerful hardware than was previously available, 
online transaction design CAN now feasibly incorporate more than it did in 
the past.

In essence, the work done by the batch process is 'spread' over the the time 
that multiple transactions run, with the difference that only events which 
cause a change will trigger the accrual. (There is a flaw in this approach 
when it comes to processes like payroll and interest, where the fact that 
time has elapsed (with or without transactions) requires calculation to 
reflect that time has elapsed. However, there are a number of ways in which 
that flaw can be remedied, so batch processing can still be obviated.

If everything is done by transactions that only occur when something needs 
to be changed, the overall processing cycles consumed will be less than the 
case where batch processes are run that look at every object to determine 
whether it changed or not, then process it if it did.

That was my point.

Pete.
```

```
In article <500103F1dtdh9U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
>"Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote in message 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>An online transaction does that, where values of A and B = 'now'.

When I hear something like this in a meeting my first response is to walk 
out... and I usually manage to squelch it.

My second response is to laugh and say 'Oh... *someone's* been getting 
their system-design from Airline Magazines again, haven't they?'... and I 
usually manage to squelch *that*, too.

My third response is a furrowed brow, a smile and then 'Of course... 
Newton came up with a similar concept for his foundation of the calculus, 
in the Principia Mathamematica, when he dealt with the prime and ultimate 
ratios to deal with nascent and evanascent quantities.  You do realise, of 
course, that this completely and utterly neglects such difficulties as a 
head's search speeds and limitations imposed by the data path width of a 
system, correct?'...

... and I *don't* usually manage to squelch that, and it usually leads to 
the bloviator turning read and saying 'No, you Just Don't Understand...' 
... and then I don't get invited to other meetings and that suits me just 
fine, thankee.

>
>As history is simply a collection of 'now' moments, batch processing can be 
>obviated by extending the scope of the transaction to do accrual. 

Batch processing cannot be obviated (in the sense of 'to make 
unnecessary') as long as there are Corner Office Idiots who demand that 
reports be on their desks (or, within the past few years, viewable on 
their terminals) by a certain time on a certain date... and such Corner 
Office Idiots often have behind them - in the case of, say, tax reporting 
or Corporate Position Statements - the Force of Law.  I've never tried 
telling a State Auditor 'Oh, the quarterlies were obviated by considering 
the intervening time to be a series of Eternal Nows'... I'd be interested 
in hearing your experiences in having done so.

DD
```

```
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> system, correct?'...

It was a non-rigourous statement. "Now" was used loosely. Of course there 
would be a series of successive nows while the transaction processed, but 
all time is relative, and to a human observer, the time a disk head takes to 
move is not easily discernable.

The point was that the "now" in which the transaction completes is a shorter 
series of "now"s than those accumulated by the Batch window overnight...

>
> ... and I *don't* usually manage to squelch that, and it usually leads to
> the bloviator turning read and saying 'No, you Just Don't Understand...'
> ... and then I don't get invited to other meetings and that suits me just
> fine, thankee.

>>
>>As history is simply a collection of 'now' moments, batch processing can 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> in hearing your experiences in having done so.

No doubt you would... :-)

Triggered transactions and stored procedures,  in conjunction with a 
transactional approach to system design, can, theoretically, produce the 
same results as batch processing, within the same time constraints. Batch 
processing is not the ONLY way to get time sensitive reports and legally 
required documents produced  within the constraints required by your COI.

Having said that, it is as good a way as any, in terms of what is currently 
viable. My position in this discussion is that such may not ALWAYS be the 
case.

Perhaps if you read the whole thread...?

Or then again...perhaps not... :-)

Pete.
```

```
Just a point here - what may be causing confusion is that batch processing 
is every bit as transactional as on-line near-real-time processing. In batch 
processing, all the transactions are just batched together. ;)

The point is I don't think we are talking about the difference between 
'batch' and 'transactional' processing so much as between 'batch' and 
'on-line' transactions.

In the first case, I think there is little or no meaning, while in the 
second is something that you might have a really good theory you are 
developing.

-Paul
```

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:501p1gF1e53ctU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> <docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message 
> news:enfuje$r2q$1@reader2.panix.com...
>> In article <500103F1dtdh9U1@mid.individual.net>,
>> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

<snip>
>
> Triggered transactions and stored procedures,  in conjunction with a 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> required documents produced  within the constraints required by your COI.

This is how the online part of our system works (but without the triggers as 
we still use Datacomm DB. I suspect if we move to DB2 we would use 
triggers.) We use MQM triggers and save the messages/transactions in a db 
and they are processed by "batch" CICS programs. By batch I mean the 
COBOL/CICS programs that are not associated with any terminal. At the end of 
the process replies are written to DB tables and MQM is triggered to send 
the replies back to the original sender. This approach does place some 
software development/acquisition burden on the organizations that interface 
with our system but we are the federal government and while they have input 
they don't have much choice.

Anyway we are able to process enough transactions quickly enough that the 
people who would like to us SAP to replace our system feel mightily 
challenged. The Java folks likewise.  There has been some talk of 
benchmarking Java against COBOL/CICS before deciding whether to use it to 
replace COBOL/CICS, but not much actual work has been done that I know of. I 
think they just want to be able to say that the old/obsolete COBOL has been 
replaced by the modern Java and I believe other factors do not matter too 
much. Well I could say a lot more but every time I start on this I remind 
myself that I am just short of retirement and I self censor myself. Besdies 
whatever their shortcomings it is still my duty to support management 
decisions.

We still have a few batch programs e.g. one copies old data from production 
to an archive system, something users probably would not like to do one at a 
time.

I am eligible to retire as of June this year and my financial advisor says I 
can afford to do it as soon as 2008. I am thinking of trying to last until 
Jan. 3, 2014 but I will just see how things go. I look forward to doing the 
projects that I want to do for a change.
```

```
"Robert Jones" <rjones0@hotmail.com> wrote in message 
news:1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com...
>
> message snipped
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>
You may be right. I concede that it is arguable.

All of the examples cited above could be produced as by-products of 
transactional processing. Payrolls, certainly are good examples of a batch 
process, but the same effect can be obtained by time-triggered processes 
against a database.

(Imagine a payroll system where a stored procedure is triggered 
automatically to calculate the base pay for an individual,  and the tax to 
be paid, and transactions that make adjustments to this have been enqueued 
against the individual account. The whole process will run automatically and 
all the paymaster has to do is provide the adjustment transactions in real 
time. It doesn't HAVE to be batch, although, in essence, it is a 
"batch-like" process, inasmuch as many people will have their pay 
calculations trggered at the same time...)

Stored procedures and Data Warehousing, together, can obviate all the batch 
processes I can think of. (I may have missed some... :-))  Printing cheques 
will soon be a thing of the past; direct transfers and smart cards are more 
secure and more convenient for the drawer and the payee.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T08:05:33-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net> <1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`

```
Typically batch programs are useful when we want the job to run for a
particular moment in time.

Closing, payroll, billing, grades, etc. need to be run for particular
times.

Traditionally, we made sure transactions would not get posted while we
ran these period-end jobs.     But more and more, we do not want to do
this.   When someone pays us via the Web in the middle of the night,
we want him to check his new balance.

One solution is to have a duplicate of our data that they can see
their updates - and merge the two databases when we are done.

Of course data warehouses can carry this duplication to the extreme.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-03T10:47:32+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50027mF1cs5dgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net> <1167574958.426550.272040@n51g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:spskp2trujv9racfriqbphc4l384s0gurs@4ax.com...
> Typically batch programs are useful when we want the job to run for a
> particular moment in time.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Of course data warehouses can carry this duplication to the extreme.

Yes, more and more people want to see how things are NOW. If the computer 
system models the real world, and deals with events as they occur, I believe 
this can be achieved, without batch processing. Sure there are time points 
where we want to see consolidation, and there are historical and traditional 
reasons (like monthly billing) where people are used to seeing the state of 
their account and so forth, that press system designers to produce the goods 
at a certain time. But it doesn't HAVE to be that way. Since going to online 
statements from my bank, for example, I haven't felt compelled to check my 
balance at the end of each month. Instead, I can check it daily  (24/7) or 
whenever I feel it needs checking...

Provided you have security and backups in place, I see no reason why you 
should stop Transaction Processing to get figures for a given moment in 
time. Instead, simply schedule a trigger for that moment (maybe weeks, 
months or even years in advance and set to repeat at whatever cycle you 
want) and let the system tell you the state of things at that moment. I 
wouldn't run duplicate databases (except in the sense of RAIDing them, which 
is transparent to the process) and I see no reason why customers can't see 
their balance in real time on the real database. It is only because the 
batch process takes time, that it is necessary to stop processing while it 
goes on. If everything was accrued already there'd be no need for a batch 
process...:-)

I realise that with existing systems in place it is not likely that 
something like this could be attained; the real world requires real 
solutions, but the concept could be used for future design.

I honestly believe that things will evolve this way, but time will tell...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-01T15:22:28-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rYemh.19469$ky2.5651@newsfe21.lga>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
<grin> Well., you are welcome to your opinion Pete, and you might even be 
right. However, I think you are looking at it from an idealized viewpoint. 
I;ve worked with some very large data warehouses, and the input data always 
requires some kind of preprocessing. In the scenario you are talking about, 
I'm not sure what you have is a real data warehouse and not some kind of 
hybrid OLTP system with a very large database on the backend.

Batch systems are rarely 'brute force' systems these days, and almost always 
operate on a set of transactions. Operations occur only on a subset of the 
main datastore, and even then the process is usually streamlined in terms of 
queries or other data access.

When you look at it from an Ops manager point of view, a batch process does 
not have to deal with transaction processing overhead, and you can usually 
eliminate any slowdowns  - like filesharing. There are many other reasons of 
course, but in simple fact, a tuned batch process *is* just that - tuned to 
do what it is designed to do with maximum speed and minimum resources.

And when you are putting stuff in a processing window, speed counts more 
than anything else except accuracy. Another advantage is that a batch system 
can run without affecting the online systems, leaving them up and able to 
process at full speed, or at best, down for only a short period of time 
while batch data is being gathered or organized.

Throwing hardware at this problem quickly reaches limits - either 
technological or financial. Usually financial. ;)  However well designed 
highly efficient batch processing is doable, can be easily controlled, is 
not difficult to expand, and is still faster.

-Paul


"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
…[83 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
"P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
news:YNmmh.28895$_44.14368@newsfe23.lga...
> Comments interspersed below...
>
<snip>>>>
>>> When you look at it from an Ops manager point of view, a batch process 
>>> does not have to deal with transaction processing overhead, and you can 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

So, then it couldn't be eliminated. My question was, if it COULD be 
eliminated... :-)

Maybe the degree of "could' depends on how badly you want to do it...



>>>
>>> And when you are putting stuff in a processing window, speed counts more 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> any kind of interactive processing was expensive.

There was a time (and I remember being part of it) when batch processing was 
the ONLY option. There WERE no online transactions. So I understand what you 
are saying. However, new advances in hardware (the provision of random 
access facilities, for a start...) enabled new approaches to be taken, and 
they were.

If you are dealing with systems like these, which certainly had OLTP "tacked 
on" to them, then I can see why you believe the way you do.

The fact is that OLTP systems designed from the ground up CAN be built 
without need for batch processing.  But it will be sometime before people 
realise they can... :-)


>And the systems are large Pete - even a very fast PC would not be suitable 
>for a lot of this kind of processing. Not because the raw processing power 
>isn't there, but because when you add the I/O systems, backup, security, 
>encryption, and Transaction Processing capability, they come up missing on 
>a a PC platform.

I strongly disagree on this... these things are certainly NOT missing on a 
PC platform. Your mainframe salesman just wants you to think they are... :-)

1. The power of the "PC platform" is not in its hardware but in its 
connectivity. It is the power of the NETWORK with distributed processing, 
remote procedure calls, load levelling and very fast message switching, that 
empowers the OLTP system. All of the things you mention above are no longer 
the preserve of the mainframe; any good client/server networked system will 
include them.

2. Networked systems built around the "PC platform" are scalable. And for 
much less money than the cost of scaling a centralized mainframe. For 
mainframes to survive, the cost of ownership will have to declne 
dramatically, and the system environment will need to become much simpler.

That has been my experience, but I accept that it is arguable and views will 
differ, depending on what you're used to and familiar with. My experience 
covers decades in both environments. I am persuaded that the role of 
mainframes in the future will be as servers on the network and Batch 
processors until everyone wises up... :-)


> By PC I am referring to the personal machines we all use - not Intel based 
> mainframes like Unisys puts out.
>

My points above were deliberately constrained to meet those criteria, with 
the exception that some of these platforms will be servers...

>
>>> Throwing hardware at this problem quickly reaches limits - either 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> :)

We ain't seen nothin' yet... :-)

Pete.
```

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:4vulmlF1e1bngU1@mid.individual.net...
>
> "P. Raulerson" <paul.rl@raulersons.com> wrote in message 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

<grin> Remind to tell you a funny story about having to argue with IBM to 
get them to sell me a mainframe a few years ago...
I won. :)

> 1. The power of the "PC platform" is not in its hardware but in its 
> connectivity. It is the power of the NETWORK with distributed processing, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
This is true, but it is not always true in all situations. Networks have 
latency issues, and queue issues, and a whole bunch of issues that we all 
battled with when Sun came up with 'the network IS the computer'. In other 
words, I don't disagree entirely.

> 2. Networked systems built around the "PC platform" are scalable. And for 
> much less money than the cost of scaling a centralized mainframe. For 
> mainframes to survive, the cost of ownership will have to declne 
> dramatically, and the system environment will need to become much simpler.
>
Oh - they *have*. For instance we run a mainframe with no full time system 
programmer - just little old me. Of course, it mainly runs Linux instances, 
but run it does and very well. And cheaply too!  You can also buy mainframes 
now that are dedicated to running particular types of things - for example 
with a processor that is tuned and optimized to run Java.

Revisting cost - you can pick up a really good mainframe system for $500K 
today, including SAN, Network, Install, OS, and three years of maintenance.

A Blade Server that is roughly equivalent, with SAN, runs closer to $730K. 
(Just bought one of them things, and I only bought 11 blades, not the full 
14. ) Software costs a LOT on the PC platforms.  In our case, about $1300 
per user for Microsoft software. That's without a database system other than 
Access.
Those darned CALS add up quick...

The big difference? Take Tivoli, for example. Tivoli can near max out a 
mainframe IFL processor without even trying, but it is a whole different 
thing trying to max out the channel I/O.  The PC is swimming in MIPS, but 
even in the newest IBM blade center chassis, has lots of trouble with I/O. 
Technically, the bandwidth is there, but compared to the mainframe, the 
components- Fibre Cards, DASD controllers, Network Controllers and such are 
poorly integrated. It really shows up too.

Yesterday I found problems on the Blade Fibre cards relating to the cards 
building up hughe queuing delays. That would simply not happen on FICON. 
(Okay, FICON is basically Token Ring on Glass - but boy, it can move data 
around.)


> That has been my experience, but I accept that it is arguable and views 
> will differ, depending on what you're used to and familiar with. My 
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
Hi Charlie,

comments below...


"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote in message 
news:1kCmh.5782$pQ3.319@newsread4.news.pas.earthlink.net...
>
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I downloaded Visual C# 2005 Express Edition  for FREE from: 
http://msdn.microsoft.com/vstudio/express/

It installs easily and the Visual Studio IDE comes up when you start it.

There is a "News" section on the start page and it has a link for "Absolute 
Beginners" I took that and started from lesson 01.

OK, for some of this course, there was a lot of stuff I already knew, but I 
tried to treat it as a new subject in a new unit of time without imposing 
ITSAs on it. As a result, I acquired of lot of useful knowledge in passing. 
These online tutorials are absolutely excellent. The base course has around 
14 hours that you can download or simply view, and the lessons are organized 
into easy pieces which I found were just the right length. In addition to 
the newbie course, there are over 400 videos you can view for free on all 
aspects of the language and the environment.

The next thing is to get stuck in and start using it. I found the help files 
to be comprehensive and the How To sections are very useful. It is really 
nice to have help that offers sample code, in the language you are actually 
writing (ever had to translate VB examples into COBOL?  :-))

If you have experience with OO and particularly JavaScript, it probably 
helps, but these are certainly not pre-requisites.

So, that's it for me... no books, no video purchases, real quality tuition 
on-line when I want it, excellent reference and examples online when I want 
them, and all for free... It is no wonder this language is taking off...

Funny thing is, if Fujitsu had sold me DotNET COBOL for several thousand 
dollars, I probably wouldn't even have looked at C#. Now I'm really glad 
they didn't... Sometimes things just work out :-)

Pete.
```
