- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <4vis2tF1cbkkdU1@mid.individual.net> <pQDlh.78496$V34.25214@newsfe17.lga> <4vpn2rF1cr7poU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I'm responding to this message instead of the one you directed to me.

Pete Dashwood<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> 12/31/06 5:00 AM >>>
>
>
>(Consider an online transaction that calculates interest on an account
which 
>last had a balance change 3 weeks ago. The transaction can access a 
>collection of interest rates for the dates between then and today and 
>calculate the accrued interest; it only has to calculate for one period
(the 
>time since the last balance chage). It "knows" the balance changed because

>it is the transaction that changed it, and it wouldn't be running UNLESS
the 
>balance changed...

If I'm reading this, and your message to me, correctly are you saying that
accrued interest is only calculated when either 1) a transactions posts or
2) it's time to pay the interest to the account?

In our environment we not only accrue interest to accounts during a daily
batch process, but we also generate combined general ledger transactions
representing the interest accrued each day for all accounts.  This, I
believe, is so that on any given day one can look at the "interest owed" GL
account and know how much interest we owe our customers, but we have not yet
actually paid to them.

Can you work this in to your "online processing"?  (Probably you can!)

I'm also still unsure as to how you would handle statement processing.  Not
the regular transactions themselves, but all of the things that are done
when the 'statement cycles', such as paying accrued interest in to the
account, assessing a monthly service charge based on numbers of transactions
during the statement period, etc.  How does the "cycle statement today"
event get triggered?  At the very least, it seems to me, there has to be a
daily event of some sort that says "trigger the 'cycle statement today'
event for all accounts scheduled to cycle today".  Seems to me that that
would "flood" your online system with what is essentailly a batch process.

Anyway, we too have an online banking site where you can see transactions
that have posted up through the last business day.  And we even have "up to
the second" pending transactions available for things like ATM and Debit
Card transactions, telephone and online initiated account transfers, etc.

Frank


--- 
Frank Swarbrick
Senior Developer/Analyst - Mainframe Applications
FirstBank Data Corporation - Lakewood, CO  USA

There are overheads with non-compiled languages. The classic example is
Basic with the interpreter being very slow (GW-Basic and MSBasic). Some
other languages interpret the queries once only (ie compile on the fly)
and have smaller overheads. I would love to see exactly how a modern
query language performs with a massive query (large volumes of data and
large numbers of fields/cells).

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T11:31:10+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com...
>
> LX-i wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Basic with the interpreter being very slow (GW-Basic and MSBasic).

Perhaps it is some time since you used these? There is nothing slow about 
modern interpreted Basic, running on a modern processor.  Visual Basic is 
compiled nowadays.


> Some
> other languages interpret the queries once only (ie compile on the fly)
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Have you tried running SQL queries against an ACCESS database on your own 
system?  Access 2003 performs very well. (It uses the SQLServer engine). I 
use MySQL and find the SQL runs very acceptably. Queries that return 
thousands of rows still run very quickly. (A query that returned 50,000 rows 
just ran in under 3 seconds, and that includes all the overheads of setup 
and connection; had the DBMS already been loaded it would have been much 
faster.)

I'm currently using C# to access databases using OLEDB and the Dot NET 
framework. A connection is made, the query runs, and results are loaded into 
a dataset, then the connection is dropped and processing proceeds with the 
local dataset. The whole thing (for the queries I have done so far, which 
are not extreme), appears to be instantaneous. However I am running on dual 
core2 64 processors so it may not be representative of things in general.

The point is that with modern processors and modern DBMS, performance is 
much less of a problem than it was a few years ago.

The approaches are different now as well. Instead of cursor processing 
through the DBMS  for queries that return many rows, the whole thing is 
loaded to a result set which is an object with its own methods and 
properties. These can be invoked without even affecting the DBMS at all, so 
stepping forward and backward or even randomly into the result set is 
achieved on the local copy. The DBMS is free to service other requests. If 
update is required it is done by a method of the result set and if 
reconnection is necessary, the result set object does it automatically, 
applies the update, and disconnects again, all transparent to the 
application program.Connections are established and held for a fraction of 
the time that used to be the case.(You can force the connection to persist 
if you have some pressing reason to do so; so far, I have been very happy 
with the results of letting the object manage this for me.)

I remember having a hard time here some years back, attempting to persuade 
people that DBMS was a better solution than the COBOL file system. My main 
reason for believing that was because the COBOL file system is closed; you 
need to write a COBOL program to access data with it. The DBMS is open and 
can be imported and exported to other standard tools. With increasing need 
for data to be manipulated and shared openly, DBMS has established itself as 
the repository of choice. The main "objections" (usually from COBOL people 
who feel threatened by having a non-COBOL solution...) have been 
"performance" and "overhead".  In today's environment, neither of these is a 
real problem; even embedded SQL (which was a much better solution than 
ISAM/VSAM KSDS file systems), is now being superseded by Object Oriented 
DBMS handling, as outlined above.

I just had a look through some of my stuff and the last time I used an 
Indexed Sequential file was 12 years ago...:-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2007-01-02T15:32:29-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<xjzmh.63598$Rg5.539356@weber.videotron.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <1167762300.998062.22940@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1167762300.998062.22940@42g2000cwt.googlegroups.com...
>I guess that you've recovered from the hangover.
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> Glad to hear it. (no sarcasm intended)

    All things being equal, a program running via an interpreter will be 
slower than the same program running pre-compiled. However...

    (1) In real life scenarios, for user applications at least (I'm not that 
familiar with the batch processing world), the CPU is typically idle, 
waiting for the user to do something. For example, my newsgroup client is 
spending most of its time, idly waiting for me to type the next character in 
my reply-post; And so processing time isn't such a big deal. We have plenty.

    (2) "All things" are not equal: A program which is interpreted can take 
advantage of information which is available at runtime, but not which was 
not available at compile time, to make better optimization decisions. Some 
program designs can take advantage of this a lot, with the interpreted 
program running faster than the compiled one. Others, not so much, and so 
the compiled program will run faster than the interpreted one.

[...]
> I can't wait for the quantum computer
> to become a reality - every answer instantly.

    Actually, there's a fairly limited class of problems for which we know 
how to program a quantum computers to solve instantly (i.e. quantum 
computers may be good for other problems as well, but we haven't figured out 
how to use them more efficiently than classical computers yet):

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computing
<quote>
   1. The only way to solve it is to guess answers repeatedly and check 
them,
   2. There are n possible answers to check,
   3. Every possible answer takes the same amount of time to check, and
   4. There are no clues about which answers might be better: generating 
possibilities randomly is just as good as checking them in some special 
order.

An example of this is a password cracker that attempts to guess the password 
for an encrypted file
</quote>

    And even then, there are still limitations in RAM, harddisk and network 
speeds.

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Productivity

- **From:** "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>

>DBMS handling, as outlined above.

On PC and UNIX systems, indexed files and record sequential files are
add-ons.  On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
manufacturers, what you refer to as the COBOL file system is actually
a native file system open and available to all tools on the box in
question.  Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
full advantage of the capabilities of the underlying file system and
in some cases, assembler sub-routines are needed.  Utilities
(sometimes I called them futilities) such as sort and various copy
programs, virtually all of the third party products such as DYL280 and
Easytrieve,  and virtually all of the languages had native access. The
one major brain dead exception was that the interactive tool -
TSO/ISPF steadfastly refused to recognize VSAM.  
>

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL file system was Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-01-07T15:02:44+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<50b2m5F1f98mjU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com>`

```

"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
news:ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 2 Jan 2007 11:31:10 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> add-ons.

Not if you buy a COBOL compiler. COBOL standards require support for the 
"COBOL file system" (Not my words... check the COBOL manual :-))

> On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
> manufacturers, what you refer to as the COBOL file system is actually
> a native file system open and available to all tools on the box in
> question.

Yes, inasmuch as VSAM is provided and many of the standard tools can take 
advantage of it. COBOL just happens to use VSAM/KSDS for indexed sequential 
support and VSAM/RRDS for relative addressing support. (I dont think 
VSAM/ESDS is used by the COBOL file system, but I could be wrong and ESDS is 
certainly accessible form COBOL if you use extensions. This may have 
changed; it is around 20 years since I worked in that environment.) The 
relative byte addressing provided by ESDS remains a mystery to most COBOL 
programmers BECAUSE it is NOT part of the "COBOL file system".

No, inasmuch as there is no standard import and export of VSAM datasets into 
formats acceptable to applications not written in COBOL, PL-1, or Assembler, 
or in cross platform formats like XML or .CSV. (Again, this may have changed 
and perhaps some of these things may be availabe now; if so it was really 
too late... we needed it 20 years ago...:-))

Because RDBMS contain details of the structure of their data they have 
ALWAYS been able to import and export structure and data into formats 
accessible by virtually any application. (at the lowest level they can 
export SQL information as text; MySQL does this for both structure and 
content and can therefore easily be imported into any other RDBMS that 
supports standard SQL. (I don't know of any that don't... :-))

The closest approximation I can think of is using IDCAMS to export the data 
on a KSDS to a sequential file, but that is a long way from "open" access.

It is probably being a bit unfair to the mainframe environment to say the 
file formats are not open, because there is little requirement for 
"openness" on a central platform. It is only as the need for networking and 
remote computing capability  across different platforms becomes more 
important that "exchangeability" of data becomes a consideration. My 
argument was (and is...) that RDBMS supports this better than the COBOL file 
system. When I first proposed this it was heresy and I was castigated in 
this forum (I still remember it... :-)... I couldn't beleive the venom in 
some of the posts...), since then the world has voted with its feet and few 
applications DON'T use RDBMS (or Hierarchical or Network DBMS) on both 
manframes and PCs.

> Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
> full advantage of the capabilities of the underlying file system and
> in some cases, assembler sub-routines are needed.  Utilities
> (sometimes I called them futilities)

 :-) (I like that...)

> such as sort and various copy
> programs, virtually all of the third party products such as DYL280 and
> Easytrieve,  and virtually all of the languages had native access. The
> one major brain dead exception was that the interactive tool -
> TSO/ISPF steadfastly refused to recognize VSAM.

yeah, I know... :-) How crazy is that? (I think it is OK if you deal with 
cataloged sequential datasets... and GDSes... not sure now...)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL file system was Re: Productivity

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2007-01-11T21:25:50+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<619dq29odei7fnplcj9ps0stp6q2er4te0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4v33ipF1a006cU1@mid.individual.net> <73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET> <4vgl8dF1bmbn5U1@mid.individual.net> <dnn7p2lh7524u11iuvt77dcchh76c53cfk@4ax.com> <9493c$4597e17f$454920f8$31548@KNOLOGY.NET> <1167651660.706212.71290@s34g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <4vtgdhF1cvbimU1@mid.individual.net> <ra6tp2d4j8j7udbg1sdehncvn5clcub98l@4ax.com> <50b2m5F1f98mjU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sun, 7 Jan 2007 15:02:44 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Clark F Morris" <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote in message 
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>"COBOL file system" (Not my words... check the COBOL manual :-))

Here is an interesting situation.  For the mainframes I mentioned, the
COBOL file system was mapped to the standard file facilities and thus
was not viewed as a COBOL file system.  For PCs and UNIX based
systems, the COBOL file system became an add-on.  For the mainframe,
the basic FORTRAN and C style files were the oddballs (delimiter
terminated strings and arrays).  The reverse is true for the PC and
UNIX systems.
>
>> On IBM mainframes and I would assume those from other
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>programmers BECAUSE it is NOT part of the "COBOL file system".

The VSAM Entry Sequenced Data Set is accessible if the SELECT
statement is coded properly.  There are heretics like myself who
believe that it should have been enhanced over time so that it could
supplant the QSAM (queued sequential access method) data sets, that
you should be able to concatenate ESDS and QSAM data sets and that you
shouldn't need a special SELECT statement to use them.
>
>No, inasmuch as there is no standard import and export of VSAM datasets into 
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>manframes and PCs.

On a PC, the RDBMS is a better choice than adding a proprietary
indexed file system.  However, there are interesting drawbacks.  I was
surprised when testing something in DB2 on the mainframe to find that
I couldn't mix accessing production (or common test) tables in a read
only manner with the test tables I was interested in within the same
program.  It seems that all of the tables have to be accessed by the
same instance of the data base subsystem.  This becomes less of a
problem as DASD becomes cheaper but it still can add up if an
application requires a large number of tables.  I am used to just
changing the file that the DD (data set definition?) statement points
to when I want my own copy of a file.  While a RDBMS makes a number of
complex situations easier and probably is better for 99.9 percent of
the customer files, it makes simple table files where there is a key
and one or two data fields much more of a pain to handle.  I suspect
that in many cases, the RDBMS has become the 40 ton truck used where a
wheelbarrow would be perfectly adequate.  An RDBMS will have greater
overhead because access is not at the row level but at the column
level based on the generated call statements that I have seen.  This
allows for interesting compression and other techniques to minimize
disk and in storage buffer space at the cost of long access pat
lengths.  The cost can be more than justified if the suite of
applications using the data base is taking advantage of the RDBMS
capabilities as opposed to treating it is a tricky indexed file.    
>
>> Indeed, on the IBM MVS / z/OS systems, COBOL doesn't take
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>cataloged sequential datasets... and GDSes... not sure now...)

I think that ISPF without tweaking can handle UNIX file systems if the
user has UNIX access allowed in their security system definition. VSAM
still is primarily through third party products.  Of course, IBM needs
to understand that just because its first attempts at providing full
GUI workstation access received underwhelming acceptance in the field,
it is no reason to retreat from the idea of providing a good modern
IDE.  
>
>Pete. 
>
```

Pete Dashwood wrote:
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
> news:73e30$4592adbc$454920f8$28313@KNOLOGY.NET...
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> in place.

But, as the systems atrophy, there are users who see the system as, at 
best, useless - and, at worst, something that actually makes their job 
harder, instead of easier.  With money having to be split between 
moderately improving old systems and these new-and-improved 
"replacement" systems, who looks out for the users?

When I arrived here, we were "frozen" on new development.  We couldn't 
even fix big bugs, because a replacement system's requirements had been 
written on one version of software.  For over 3 years, there were no 
improvements to our system, and the users were howling.  Once that 
contract lapsed (120M USD later), we were able to make great strides. 
Did they "learn their lesson"?  Of course not.  Now we're hearing 
rumblings about the same thing happening again.

> why don't you write a paper on the system, outling some of the areas that 
> could be improved , and suggesting some approaches so that anyone taking 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Even if they don't, at least you know you gave it your best shot.

That's my plan.  In fact, there's one particular process that I'm going 
to do a loose design and code on as a training task for the guy who is 
going to be picking up most of my programs.
```
